# PhysicsMind — Complete Concept & Simulation Catalogue

> **For physics-teacher review.** This is the full atomic + nano concept inventory across all 44 curriculum topics (NCERT Class 11 + 12, JEE/NEET).
> Each row is one **teachable idea = one planned interactive simulation**. The `Requires` / `Required-by` columns are the dependency graph — use them to design the teaching pathway (what to teach before what).

**What we'd love your help with:** for each concept — and especially the 💎 *diamond* moments — how would you teach it? What's the cleanest order, the right "aha" demonstration, and the wrong belief students walk in with that the simulation must break?

**Status note:** this is the *plan*, not what's built yet. ~65 simulations are built so far; the catalogue is the full roadmap of everything we intend to build, sourced from where NCERT + HC Verma + DC Pandey all cover a concept. Topic numbers skip a few (5, 24, 28, 32, 33, 40) — those are either built directly without a catalogue (e.g. Vectors) or out of scope for now.

## How to read this

| Column | Meaning |
|---|---|
| **Type — atomic** | One complete teachable idea = one student question = one simulation (e.g. *free fall under gravity*). |
| **Type — nano** | The smallest sub-unit — one symbol, one term, one edge-case under a parent atomic (shown with `↳`). |
| **Simulatable** | ✅ = the idea can be shown as an interactive/animated simulation. (Almost all can.) |
| **Diamond** | A high-impact "aha" simulation worth building to the highest polish — the moments that create understanding. |
| **Targets misconception** | The specific wrong belief students hold that this simulation is designed to correct. |
| **Requires** | Concepts a student should meet *before* this one — the incoming edges of the learning pathway. |
| **Required-by** | Concepts that build on this one — the outgoing edges. Together these define the teaching sequence. |
| **Description** | What the simulation actually teaches — the physics content, including derivation/formula where relevant. |

## Summary

- **Topics:** 45
- **Total concept-simulations:** 1563  ( 727 atomic + 836 nano )
- **Unique concept IDs:** 1561  *(some concepts are shared across more than one topic)*
- **Flagged simulatable:** 1417
- **Diamond (highest-impact) simulations:** 63

## T1 — Physical World  *(Class 11)*

*3 atomic + 8 nano = 11 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **fundamental_forces_in_nature_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The **four fundamental forces**: gravitational, electromagnetic, strong nuclear, weak nuclear. Relative strengths (~1 : 10⁻² : 10⁻¹³ : 10⁻³⁸, taking strong = 1) and ranges (gravity + EM infinite; strong ~10⁻¹⁵ m; weak ~10⁻¹⁸ m). EVERY force in nature is one of these four. | — (umbrella) | [field_forces+contact_forces(T11), gravitation(T16), moving_charges(T36), nuclear_force(T48)] | dozens of different forces |
| ↳ four_forces_strength_range_table_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The comparison table: strong (1, ~10⁻¹⁵ m), EM (10⁻², infinite), weak (10⁻¹³, ~10⁻¹⁸ m), gravity (10⁻³⁸, infinite). **Simulatable as a strength-bar + range-scale visual.** Why gravity dominates cosmically (infinite range + always attractive) despite being weakest. | [fundamental_forces_in_nature_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ all_contact_forces_are_em_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Friction, tension, normal reaction, spring force, drag — ALL are the electromagnetic repulsion/attraction between the electron clouds of surface atoms. **The single most unifying aha**: the macroscopic contact-force zoo of T11/T12 is ONE force (EM) in disguise. Genuine "small → smaller" recursion. | [fundamental_forces_in_nature_atomic] | [contact_forces(T11), friction_static_kinetic(T12)] |  |
| ↳ unification_story_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The drive toward unification: Maxwell unified electricity + magnetism + optics into electromagnetism; the electroweak theory unified EM + weak; the search for grand unification + quantum gravity continues. Physics seeks the fewest principles explaining the most phenomena. | [fundamental_forces_in_nature_atomic] | — |  |
| **conservation_laws_as_organizing_principles_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Conservation of **energy, linear momentum, angular momentum, and charge** as the DEEP organizing principles of physics — rooted in symmetries of nature (qualitative Noether: time-symmetry → energy; space-symmetry → momentum; rotational-symmetry → angular momentum). More fundamental than any specific force law: they hold even when the detailed forces are unknown. | — (umbrella) | [work_energy(T13), momentum_collisions(T14), rotational(T15), electric_charge(T29)] | conservation law = just one more formula |
| ↳ conservation_from_symmetry_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Qualitative Noether: every conservation law mirrors a symmetry of nature. Energy ← laws don't change over time; momentum ← laws don't change across space; angular momentum ← laws don't change with orientation. **Why conservation is DEEP, not arbitrary.** | [conservation_laws_as_organizing_principles_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ conservation_makes_problems_solvable_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Conservation laws let you relate "before" and "after" WITHOUT tracking the messy forces in between (e.g. collision: momentum before = momentum after, no need to integrate the contact force). This is WHY they are the physicist's first tool. | [conservation_laws_as_organizing_principles_atomic] | [momentum_collisions(T14), work_energy(T13)] |  |
| **scope_and_domains_of_physics_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The reach of physics across **scale** (10⁻¹⁵ m nucleus → 10⁺²⁶ m observable universe, ~41 orders of magnitude) and the **three domains of validity**: CLASSICAL (everyday sizes/speeds), QUANTUM (atomic/sub-atomic), RELATIVISTIC (speeds near c). Each theory is the correct model WITHIN its domain. | — (umbrella) | [order_of_magnitude(T2), all-quantitative-physics (conceptual)] | classical mechanics is wrong, replaced by quantum/relativity |
| ↳ scales_of_the_universe_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The scale ladder: quark < proton (10⁻¹⁵ m) < atom (10⁻¹⁰) < human (10⁰) < Earth (10⁷) < solar system (10¹³) < galaxy (10²¹) < observable universe (10²⁶). Links to T2 order-of-magnitude estimation. **Simulatable as a logarithmic zoom.** | [scope_and_domains_of_physics_atomic, dimensional_analysis_atomic(T2)] | — |  |
| ↳ domains_of_validity_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Classical mechanics is EXACT for everyday scales/speeds (a cricket ball, a car); quantum is needed at atomic scale; relativity at speeds near c. The boundaries (not right-vs-wrong) — classical is a limiting case of both. | [scope_and_domains_of_physics_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ physics_technology_society_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Physics → technology → society loop: ISRO (orbital mechanics + EM), DAE nuclear power (nuclear forces), semiconductor/IT (quantum). The Indian national-science narrative. **Marketing-relevant framing**: physics is the engine behind India's strategic capabilities. | [scope_and_domains_of_physics_atomic] | — |  |

## T2 — Units Measurements  *(Class 11)*

*5 atomic + 15 nano = 20 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The **7 SI base units** (metre, kilogram, second, ampere, kelvin, mole, candela) and how every **derived unit** (N = kg·m/s², J = N·m, ...) is built from them. **Unit consistency** is the zeroth-order check on any equation; the **factor-label method** uses units as an algebraic derivation tool. | [basic_algebra(Class 9)] | [dimensional_analysis_atomic, EVERY quantitative atomic (universal, implicit)] | units are droppable labels |
| ↳ seven_base_units_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The 7 base quantities + their SI units + symbols; every other physical quantity is derived. Why exactly 7 (independence — none derivable from the others). | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ unit_conversion_factor_label_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Convert via multiplying by 1 in disguise (e.g., 72 km/h × (1000 m/km) × (1 h/3600 s) = 20 m/s). Units cancel like algebra; the method PREVENTS conversion errors. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ si_redefinition_2019_nano | nano | ✅ |  | 2019 SI redefinition: kg now fixed via Planck constant h (no physical artefact); ampere via electron charge e; kelvin via Boltzmann k. **Modern Indian-relevant anchor**: CSIR-NPL realises the new kg with a Kibble balance. Constants, not objects, define units. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic, seven_base_units_nano] | — |  |
| **dimensional_analysis_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | The **dimensional formula** [MᵃLᵇTᶜ...] of a quantity; the **principle of homogeneity** (every additive term in a valid equation has identical dimensions). Three legitimate uses: (1) CHECK an equation; (2) CONVERT units between systems; (3) DERIVE a relation UP TO a dimensionless constant. The universal sanity-check applied implicitly across all of physics. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic] | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic, EVERY formula-bearing atomic (universal); absorbs math-tool `dimensional_analysis`+`unit_analysis` stubs] | gives complete formula incl. constants |
| ↳ principle_of_homogeneity_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Every additive term in a physically valid equation carries the SAME dimensions (you cannot add a length to a time). The instant equation-validity test: if the terms don't match dimensionally, the equation is WRONG (necessary, not sufficient). | [dimensional_analysis_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ deriving_relations_dimensionally_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Find the FORM of a relation by matching powers: T = k·mᵃ·lᵇ·gᶜ → solve a,b,c by dimensional balance → T = k√(l/g). **The k (= 2π here) is NOT obtainable** — must come from theory/experiment. The power-matching method. | [dimensional_analysis_atomic, principle_of_homogeneity_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ dimensional_limitations_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Three hard failures: (a) cannot find dimensionless constants (½, 2π, sin's coefficient); (b) cannot handle multi-term-of-unknown-form equations; (c) arguments of sin/cos/log/exp MUST be dimensionless (ωt is fine; t alone inside sin is dimensionally illegal). **The single most-tested conceptual trap.** | [dimensional_analysis_atomic] | — |  |
| **significant_figures_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The digits that carry real measurement information. Counting rules (non-zero always count; captive zeros count; leading zeros never; trailing zeros after a decimal count). The **asymmetric arithmetic rule**: +/− keeps the least DECIMAL PLACES; ×/÷ keeps the least SIG-FIG COUNT. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic] | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic] | decimal places = precision |
| ↳ sig_fig_counting_rules_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Leading zeros (0.0025 → 2 sf) vs captive zeros (1002 → 4 sf) vs trailing zeros (2.50 → 3 sf; 2500 → ambiguous, use scientific notation 2.5×10³). Scientific notation removes ambiguity. | [significant_figures_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ sig_figs_in_arithmetic_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The asymmetric rule in action: 12.11 + 0.3 = 12.4 (1 decimal place); 4.32 × 1.1 = 4.8 (2 sf). The weakest measurement caps the result's precision. | [significant_figures_atomic, sig_fig_counting_rules_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ rounding_convention_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Standard rounding (≥5 rounds up); only round the FINAL answer, never intermediate steps (intermediate rounding compounds error). Carry a guard digit. | [significant_figures_atomic] | — |  |
| **error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Absolute error** Δx, **relative error** Δx/x, **percentage error** (Δx/x)×100. Propagation: errors ADD (absolute) for sums/differences; RELATIVE errors add for products/quotients; for Z = (A^p B^q)/C^r the relative error = p(ΔA/A) + q(ΔB/B) + r(ΔC/C). The exponent MULTIPLIES the relative error. | [dimensional_analysis_atomic, significant_figures_atomic] | [EVERY experimental-result atomic (universal, implicit)] | errors always just add |
| ↳ combination_of_errors_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Derivation of the propagation rules from differentials: for Z = A·B, dZ/Z = dA/A + dB/B (relative errors add). The power rule from Z = Aⁿ: ΔZ/Z = n·(ΔA/A). | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic, derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic(T3)] | — |  |
| ↳ percentage_error_worked_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Worked: density ρ = m/V; m = 5.00 ± 0.01 g (0.2%), V = 4.0 ± 0.1 cm³ (2.5%) → Δρ/ρ = 0.2% + 2.5% = 2.7%. **The volume measurement dominates** — improving the balance is wasted effort. Error budgeting. | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic, combination_of_errors_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ least_count_sets_minimum_error_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The instrument's least count is the floor on absolute error for a single reading. A vernier reading to 0.01 cm cannot claim 0.001 cm precision. Connects instrument choice → achievable error. | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic, measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic] | — |  |
| **measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Least count** = smallest measurable division. **Vernier caliper** LC = 1 MSD − 1 VSD (typically 0.01 cm). **Screw gauge** LC = pitch / number-of-circular-divisions (typically 0.001 cm). **Zero error** (systematic; subtract it). **Accuracy** (closeness to true value) vs **precision** (repeatability/spread) — independent qualities. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic, significant_figures_atomic] | [error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic] | accuracy = precision |
| ↳ vernier_screw_gauge_reading_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Vernier: main-scale reading + (coinciding vernier division × LC). Screw gauge: main-scale + (circular-scale × LC), watch for backlash. The two workhorse Indian-physics-lab instruments. | [measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ accuracy_vs_precision_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Bullseye picture: tight cluster off-centre = precise-but-inaccurate (systematic error / zero error); scattered around centre = accurate-but-imprecise (random error). The two are independent; good measurement needs both. | [measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ zero_error_correction_nano | nano | ✅ |  | A screw gauge reading 0.002 cm with jaws closed has +0.002 cm zero error → SUBTRACT from every reading. Systematic (not random) — repeating the measurement does NOT average it out. | [measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic, accuracy_vs_precision_nano] | — |  |

## T3 — Mathematical Tools  *(Class 11)*

*4 atomic + 11 nano = 15 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The derivative dy/dx = **slope of the tangent** to the y(x) curve; d/dt = **instantaneous rate of change**. Physics meaning: v = dx/dt, a = dv/dt, I = dq/dt, P = dW/dt, F = dp/dt. The standard-derivatives table (xⁿ, sin, cos, eˣ) is downstream of this geometric/rate meaning. | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic] | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic(T6), acceleration_definition_atomic(T6), EVERY rate-defined quantity (universal); absorbs math-tool `calculus_derivative_basics`] | derivative is just a formula to memorise |
| ↳ slope_of_tangent_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Geometric meaning: the derivative at a point = the slope of the line just touching the curve there. Secant → tangent as Δx → 0. The limit definition made visual. | [derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ standard_derivatives_table_nano | nano | ✅ |  | d(xⁿ)/dx = nxⁿ⁻¹; d(sin x)/dx = cos x; d(cos x)/dx = −sin x; d(eˣ)/dx = eˣ; chain + product rule. The operational toolkit — but taught AFTER the slope/rate meaning. | [derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic, slope_of_tangent_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ derivative_in_physics_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The recurring physics derivatives: v = dx/dt (speedometer reads this), a = dv/dt, I = dq/dt, P = dW/dt, F = dp/dt. Each is a RATE the student already intuits before the calculus. | [derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic] | — |  |
| **integral_as_area_and_accumulation_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The definite integral ∫ₐᵇ f(x) dx = **area under the f(x) curve** = **accumulated total**. Physics meaning: displacement = ∫v dt (area under v-t), work = ∫F dx (area under F-x), charge = ∫I dt, impulse = ∫F dt. The **Fundamental Theorem** links it to the derivative as the inverse operation. | [derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic, functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic] | [s_in_equations(T6), work-energy atomics(T13), EVERY accumulated quantity (universal); absorbs math-tool `calculus_integration_basics`] | integration & differentiation unrelated |
| ↳ area_under_curve_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The integral as the limit of summed rectangles (Riemann sum) → exact area. For a v-t graph the area IS the displacement; for an F-x graph the area IS the work. Area carries physical meaning. | [integral_as_area_and_accumulation_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ fundamental_theorem_link_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Integration UNDOES differentiation: if v = dx/dt then x = ∫v dt + x₀. The derivative gives the rate; the integral accumulates it back. **The two operations are inverses** — the deepest conceptual point of T3. | [integral_as_area_and_accumulation_atomic, derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ integral_in_physics_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The recurring physics integrals: displacement = ∫v dt (odometer accumulates this), work = ∫F·dx, charge = ∫I dt, impulse = ∫F dt, ISRO trajectory = numerically-integrated acceleration. | [integral_as_area_and_accumulation_atomic] | — |  |
| **functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The 5 canonical physics function-shapes and how to READ them: **linear** (y = mx + c — slope m + intercept c), **quadratic** (parabola — e.g. projectile y vs x), **inverse** (1/x — e.g. Coulomb, Boyle), **sinusoidal** (A sin(ωt + φ) — SHM, waves, AC), **exponential** (e^(±t/τ) — decay/growth). Extract physics from slope, intercept, area, and curvature. | [si_units_and_unit_consistency_atomic(T2)] | [derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic, integral_as_area_and_accumulation_atomic, xt_graph+vt_graph+at_graph(T6)] | graph is just a picture |
| ↳ slope_and_intercept_reading_nano | nano | ✅ |  | From a straight-line graph: slope = Δy/Δx (with composite units, e.g. v-t slope is m/s² = acceleration); intercept = value at x = 0 (physical initial condition). Reading physics OFF the line. | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ common_physics_function_shapes_nano | nano | ✅ |  | The 5 shapes mapped to physics: linear (uniform motion), parabola (projectile/accelerated), 1/x (inverse-square fields, Boyle), sine (oscillation/wave), exponential (RC/radioactivity). Recognising the shape names the physics. | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ linearising_graphs_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Convert a curved relation to a straight line to extract constants: plot T² vs l (not T vs l) for a pendulum → slope gives g; log-log plots for power laws. **The core Indian-physics-lab data-analysis skill.** | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic, slope_and_intercept_reading_nano] | — |  |
| **approximations_in_physics_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Controlled** approximations that extract leading behaviour: **small-angle** (sin θ ≈ tan θ ≈ θ, cos θ ≈ 1 for θ ≪ 1 rad) and **binomial** ((1 + x)ⁿ ≈ 1 + nx for x ≪ 1). The WHY (keep the dominant term, drop negligible ones) and the validity boundary (~0.1 rad ≈ 6° for ~1% error). Physics is BUILT on these (SHM, paraxial optics, g at small height). | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic] | [shm_atomics(T17), paraxial_optics(T41/T42), g_at_height(T16); absorbs math-tool `trig_small_angle_approximations`+`series_binomial_expansion`] | approximations are sloppy / less correct |
| ↳ small_angle_approximation_nano | nano | ✅ |  | For θ ≪ 1 rad: sin θ ≈ θ, tan θ ≈ θ, cos θ ≈ 1 − θ²/2 ≈ 1. Validity ~0.1 rad (≈ 6°) for ~1% accuracy. The reason the pendulum is SHM ONLY for small swings. **Must use radians.** | [approximations_in_physics_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ binomial_first_order_nano | nano | ✅ |  | (1 + x)ⁿ ≈ 1 + nx for x ≪ 1 (any real n). Used for g(h) = g(1 + h/R)⁻² ≈ g(1 − 2h/R) at small height; relativistic + index-of-refraction expansions. Keep the leading correction, drop x². | [approximations_in_physics_atomic] | — |  |

## T4 — Lab Experiments  *(Class 11)*

*3 atomic + 10 nano = 13 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **graph_based_constant_determination_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | THE core experimental skill: take a physical relation, **linearise** it (choose axes so the plot is a straight line), plot the data, draw the **best-fit line**, and extract a physical constant from the **slope** (or intercept). The graph IS the measurement instrument — it averages out random scatter and exposes systematic trends. | [functions_and_graphs_in_physics_atomic(T3), derivative_as_rate_and_slope_atomic(T3), error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic(T2)] | — (terminal sink) | the graph is just for plotting marks |
| ↳ pendulum_g_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Simple pendulum: time N oscillations (reduces reaction-time error), compute T, plot **T² vs l** (a straight line through origin), slope = 4π²/g → solve for g. Amplitude must stay small (SHM validity, links to T17 + T3 small-angle). The canonical "find a constant from a slope" experiment. | [graph_based_constant_determination_atomic, free_fall_gravity_atomic(T6), approximations_in_physics_atomic(T3)] | — |  |
| ↳ youngs_modulus_searle_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Searle's apparatus: hang increasing loads on a wire, measure extension with a vernier/micrometer, plot **extension vs load** (straight line), slope + wire geometry → Young's modulus Y. Links to T18 elasticity (stress/strain). | [graph_based_constant_determination_atomic, elasticity_youngs_modulus(T18)] | — |  |
| ↳ spring_constant_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Loaded spring: plot **extension vs load** → slope = 1/k (Hooke's law); OR oscillation **T² vs mass** → slope = 4π²/k. Two routes to k; cross-check. Links to T17 SHM. | [graph_based_constant_determination_atomic, simple_harmonic_motion(T17)] | — |  |
| **measurement_instruments_practical_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The PROCEDURAL skill of reading precision instruments: **vernier caliper** (main-scale reading + coinciding-vernier-division × least count), **screw gauge** (main-scale + circular-scale × least count, watch for backlash), **spherometer** (for radius of curvature). Distinct from the least-count CONCEPT (T2) — this is the hands-on reading technique. | [measurement_instruments_least_count_atomic(T2), significant_figures_atomic(T2)] | — (terminal sink) | knowing the LC formula = being able to read it |
| ↳ vernier_reading_technique_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Read the main scale just before the zero of the vernier; find the vernier division that COINCIDES with a main-scale line; reading = MSR + (coinciding division × LC). Parallax-free viewing (eye perpendicular). | [measurement_instruments_practical_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ screw_gauge_reading_technique_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Rotate the ratchet (not the thimble) to avoid over-tightening; read main-scale + (circular-scale × LC); account for backlash error (always rotate one way). Watch for zero error (correct per T2). | [measurement_instruments_practical_atomic, zero_error_correction_nano(T2)] | — |  |
| ↳ error_sources_per_experiment_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Every experiment has characteristic error sources: pendulum (large amplitude breaks SHM; reaction-time → time many oscillations); Searle's (temperature drift, wire kinks); metre-bridge (end-corrections, jockey pressure). **Repetition cuts RANDOM error but NOT systematic error** — systematic sources must be identified + corrected separately. | [measurement_instruments_practical_atomic, error_analysis_and_propagation_atomic(T2)] | — | more readings = more accuracy |
| **circuit_and_optics_bench_experiments_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | The bench-experiment skill set: **electrical** (metre bridge + potentiometer for resistance/EMF comparison; Ohm's-law verification) and **optical** (focal length of lens/mirror by u-v method; refractive index by real/apparent-depth or by travelling microscope). Each combines an instrument-reading skill with a graph/calculation. | [graph_based_constant_determination_atomic, ohms_law(T33/T34), refraction_lenses(T42)] | — (terminal sink) |  |
| ↳ metre_bridge_resistance_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Wheatstone-bridge principle on a 1 m wire: balance point gives unknown resistance R = R_known × (l/(100−l)). End-corrections are the key systematic error (interchange known/unknown to cancel). Links to T34 Kirchhoff/Wheatstone. | [circuit_and_optics_bench_experiments_atomic, kirchhoff_wheatstone(T34)] | — |  |
| ↳ potentiometer_emf_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Potentiometer compares EMFs (or measures internal resistance) by null-deflection — no current drawn at balance, so it reads TRUE EMF (unlike a voltmeter). Balance length ∝ EMF. Links to T34. | [circuit_and_optics_bench_experiments_atomic, kirchhoff_wheatstone(T34)] | — |  |
| ↳ lens_focal_length_uv_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Lens u-v method: measure several (u, v) pairs, plot **1/v vs 1/u** (straight line) → intercepts give 1/f; OR plot v vs u and use the lens formula. Combines bench-reading + graph-method. Links to T42. | [circuit_and_optics_bench_experiments_atomic, refraction_lenses(T42), graph_based_constant_determination_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ refractive_index_real_apparent_depth_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Travelling microscope: focus on a mark, then on it through a glass slab; real depth / apparent depth = refractive index n. A direct single-measurement n. Links to T42 refraction. | [circuit_and_optics_bench_experiments_atomic, refraction_lenses(T42)] | — |  |

## T5 — Vectors  *(Class 11)*

*14 atomic + 5 nano = 19 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **unit_vector** | atomic | ✅ |  | A vector of magnitude 1 that carries direction only; any vector = (magnitude) × (unit vector). Notation â = A/\\|A\\|. The basis of all component work. | — | [vector_head_to_tail, equal_vs_parallel, vector_resolution, unit_vector_form] |  |
| **scalar_multiplication** | atomic | ✅ |  | Multiplying a vector by scalar k scales its magnitude by \\|k\\| and keeps direction if k>0, reverses it if k<0. | — | [negative_vector, parallelogram_law_test] |  |
| **negative_vector** | atomic | ✅ |  | −A is A with the same magnitude but opposite direction (the k=−1 case of scalar multiplication). A + (−A) = 0. | [scalar_multiplication] | [angle_between_vectors] |  |
| **vector_head_to_tail** | atomic | ✅ |  | Geometric (triangle) addition: place the tail of B at the head of A; the arrow from A's tail to B's head is the resultant. Generalises to the polygon law. | [unit_vector] | [resultant_formula, parallelogram_law_test] |  |
| **equal_vs_parallel** | atomic | ✅ |  | Equal vectors: same magnitude AND direction (position irrelevant). Parallel: same direction, any magnitude. Distinguishes "equal" from "parallel/collinear." | [unit_vector] | — | vectors at different points can't be equal |
| **angle_between_vectors** | atomic | ✅ |  | Protocol: bring both vectors to a common tail, arrows pointing outward; the angle between them is ≤ 180°. Critical for dot/cross products and resultant direction. | [negative_vector] | [resultant_formula, dot_product, vector_resolution] | measure the angle without moving both to one tail |
| **parallelogram_law_test** | atomic | ✅ |  | The definitive test for whether a quantity is a vector: it must add by the parallelogram (equivalently triangle) law AND obey scalar multiplication. Direction alone is not enough. | [vector_head_to_tail, scalar_multiplication] | [current_not_vector, pressure_scalar, area_vector] |  |
| **current_not_vector** | atomic | ✅ |  | Electric current has a direction (along the wire) yet is a SCALAR — at a junction currents add algebraically (Kirchhoff), not by the parallelogram law. The counterexample that defines "vector." | [parallelogram_law_test] | [drift_velocity(T34)] | anything with direction is a vector |
| **pressure_scalar** | atomic | ✅ |  | Pressure pushes in all directions at a point yet is a SCALAR; the vector is the FORCE on a chosen surface, F = P·(A n̂). Distinguishes the scalar field from the vector it generates. | [current_not_vector] | [fluid_pressure_atomic(T20)] | pressure has a direction so it's a vector |
| **area_vector** | atomic | ✅ |  | Area is a scalar (size of a surface) but can be promoted to a vector A = A n̂ (magnitude × outward normal) — the form used for flux. Two faces of one quantity. | [parallelogram_law_test] | [electric_flux(T30), magnetic_flux_definition(T35)] |  |
| **resultant_formula** | atomic | ✅ |  | Magnitude of A + B from the law of cosines: \\|R\\| = √(A² + B² + 2AB cos θ). Why scalar addition fails when θ ≠ 0. | [vector_head_to_tail, angle_between_vectors] | [special_cases, range_inequality, direction_of_resultant] |  |
| ↳ special_cases | nano | ✅ |  | The three shortcuts: θ=0° → R = A+B; θ=90° → R = √(A²+B²); θ=180° → R = \\|A−B\\|. | [resultant_formula] | — |  |
| ↳ range_inequality | nano | ✅ |  | The resultant is bounded: \\|A−B\\| ≤ R ≤ A+B. Maximum when parallel, minimum when antiparallel. | [resultant_formula] | — |  |
| ↳ direction_of_resultant | nano | ✅ |  | The resultant's direction: tan α = B sin θ / (A + B cos θ), measured from A. | [resultant_formula] | — |  |
| **vector_resolution** | atomic | ✅ |  | Splitting a vector into perpendicular components A_x = A cos θ, A_y = A sin θ; choosing axes to simplify a problem; recovering the vector by Pythagoras. The workhorse of all 2D physics. | [unit_vector, angle_between_vectors] | [inclined_plane_components, negative_components, unit_vector_form, dot_product, position_frame_of_reference_atomic(T6), sign_convention_1d_atomic(T6), vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7), projectile_decoupling_atomic(T8), free_body_diagram(T11), magnetic_force_direction_right_hand_rule(T36), antenna_range_formula(T50)] |  |
| ↳ inclined_plane_components | nano | ✅ |  | Resolving weight on a slope: mg sin θ along the incline, mg cos θ perpendicular. The canonical resolution application. | [vector_resolution] | [block_on_incline(T11), friction_block_on_inclined_plane(T12)] |  |
| ↳ negative_components | nano | ✅ |  | Components carry signs that track direction (e.g. a vector in the 2nd quadrant has negative x-component); sign ≠ size. | [vector_resolution] | — |  |
| **unit_vector_form** | atomic | ✅ |  | Writing a vector in î, ĵ, k̂ form: A = A_x î + A_y ĵ + A_z k̂. The algebraic representation behind component addition and products. | [unit_vector, vector_resolution] | [dot_product] |  |
| **dot_product** | atomic | ✅ |  | Scalar product A·B = \\|A\\|\\|B\\| cos θ = A_x B_x + A_y B_y + A_z B_z. Geometric meaning (projection/overlap); zero ⇔ perpendicular. | [vector_resolution, angle_between_vectors] | [work_done_by_constant_force(T13)] |  |

## T6 — Kinematics 1D  *(Class 11)*

*9 atomic + 17 nano = 26 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **position_frame_of_reference_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | A particle's location specified by coordinates relative to a chosen origin + axes. **Frame of reference** = the coordinate system + clock from which observations are made. Motion is always relative to a frame; "absolute" rest does not exist. | [vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | [displacement_vs_distance_atomic, relative_velocity_1d_atomic, position_frame_of_reference_atomic(T7 2D extension)] |  |
| ↳ origin_choice_arbitrary_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Choice of origin is arbitrary — physics doesn't change. **What changes**: numerical values of x, v(if moving frame), a(if accelerating frame). **What stays the same**: relative-positions, time-intervals, accelerations between inertial frames. | [position_frame_of_reference_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ inertial_vs_noninertial_frame_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **Inertial frame**: a frame in which Newton's 1st law holds (free particle moves uniformly). **Non-inertial frame**: accelerating frame (e.g., braking-car frame); pseudo-forces appear. Examples: ground-frame, train-moving-uniform-velocity = inertial; train-braking = non-inertial. | [position_frame_of_reference_atomic] | [newton_laws_atomic(T11)] |  |
| **displacement_vs_distance_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | **Displacement** Δx = x_final − x_initial (vector in 1D = signed scalar). **Distance** = path length traversed (scalar, always ≥ 0). For round-trip: displacement = 0; distance = 2·(path length). Distance ≥ \|displacement\| always. | [position_frame_of_reference_atomic, sign_convention_1d_atomic] | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic] | displacement = distance always |
| ↳ round_trip_displacement_zero_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Bengaluru→Mumbai→Bengaluru: distance = 2 × 980 km ≈ 1960 km; displacement = 0. Tabla-player travels back-and-forth between two notes: musically meaningful path; net displacement zero. **Cognitive scaffold** distinguishing scalar accumulation from vector return. | [displacement_vs_distance_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ negative_displacement_meaning_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Choose +x to the right. Object moves 3m right then 7m left: x_final − x_initial = (0+3−7) − 0 = −4 m. Negative displacement = position now LEFT of start. Sign carries direction. | [displacement_vs_distance_atomic, sign_convention_1d_atomic] | — |  |
| **avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Average velocity** v_avg = Δx/Δt over finite interval. **Instantaneous velocity** v = dx/dt = lim_{Δt→0} Δx/Δt. **Speed** = \|v\| = magnitude only (scalar). Average velocity ≠ average speed in general (round-trip: v_avg = 0 but avg speed ≠ 0). | [displacement_vs_distance_atomic, calculus_basic_derivative(math-tools)] | [acceleration_definition_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic, xt_vt_at_graphs_atomic] | v_avg = (v₁+v₂)/2 always |
| ↳ instantaneous_as_slope_of_xt_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Geometrically: v(t) at instant t = slope of x-t graph at that point. Tangent-line construction. **CBSE/ISC physics-practical lab**: students sketch x-t curves and compute slopes at chosen instants. | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, xt_vt_at_graphs_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ average_velocity_vs_average_speed_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Mumbai-Pune Expressway: drive 150 km in 3 hr (Mumbai→Pune). Then drive back 150 km in 2 hr. **Average velocity** = (0 km displacement) / (5 hr) = 0. **Average speed** = (300 km) / (5 hr) = 60 km/hr. Two different scalars; both useful, different questions. | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, displacement_vs_distance_atomic] | — |  |
| **acceleration_definition_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | **Acceleration** a = dv/dt = rate of change of velocity. Average acceleration a_avg = Δv/Δt; instantaneous a = lim_{Δt→0} Δv/Δt. **Sign carries direction**: positive a means v increasing in +x direction (if v already +); but if v is −, positive a means v becoming LESS NEGATIVE (slowing down then reversing). | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, calculus_basic_derivative(math-tools)] | [kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic, free_fall_gravity_atomic, newton_2nd_law_atomic(T11)] | negative a always means slowing down |
| ↳ sign_of_a_vs_sign_of_v_2x2_matrix_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **4 cases** (with +x as positive): (a>0, v>0) → speeding up forward; (a>0, v<0) → slowing down then reversing forward; (a<0, v>0) → slowing down then reversing backward; (a<0, v<0) → speeding up backward. **Diamond-candidate animation**: 2×2 grid showing object behaviour each case. | [acceleration_definition_atomic, sign_convention_1d_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ deceleration_vs_negative_acceleration_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **Deceleration** = magnitude of acceleration opposing velocity. **Negative acceleration** = a < 0 in chosen sign convention. These are NOT synonyms. A car moving left (v < 0) with brakes (a > 0): "decelerating" but positive a. | [acceleration_definition_atomic] | — |  |
| **kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | When acceleration is constant: **(1) v = u + at**; **(2) s = ut + ½at²**; **(3) v² = u² + 2as**. All three derive from integrating a = constant; they are three rearrangements (one for each unknown). **Use what's given.** Also **s_n = u + ½a(2n−1)** for displacement in nth second. | [acceleration_definition_atomic, avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, calculus_integration(math-tools)] | [free_fall_gravity_atomic, projectile_motion_atomic(T8)] | memorise three formulas |
| ↳ s_n_displacement_in_nth_second_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Displacement during the nth second (between t=n−1 and t=n) = u + ½a(2n−1). **Common JEE/NEET problem-template**. Derive from s_n = s(n) − s(n−1) using eq.(2). | [kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ choice_of_equation_by_given_unknown_nano | nano | ✅ |  | If given (u, a, t) → use (1) for v. If given (u, a, t) → use (2) for s. If given (u, a, s) → use (3) for v. If given (v, u, s) → use (3) for a. **Decision-tree pedagogy** rather than blind memorisation. | [kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic] | — |  |
| **xt_vt_at_graphs_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Three synchronised views of same motion: **x-t graph** position vs time; **v-t graph** velocity vs time; **a-t graph** acceleration vs time. Relationships: slope of x-t = v(t); slope of v-t = a(t); area under v-t between t₁ and t₂ = Δx; area under a-t = Δv. | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic, acceleration_definition_atomic, calculus_integration_area_under_curve(math-tools)] | [projectile_motion_atomic(T8)] |  |
| ↳ shape_decoder_table_nano | nano | ✅ |  | x-t straight line = uniform v; x-t parabola opening up = positive a; x-t curve flattening = decelerating; v-t horizontal = zero a (uniform motion); v-t straight rising = constant positive a; v-t straight falling = constant negative a. **Cognitive scaffold**: shape ↔ physical meaning table. | [xt_vt_at_graphs_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ area_under_vt_equals_displacement_nano | nano | ✅ |  | ∫_{t₁}^{t₂} v(t) dt = x(t₂) − x(t₁) = Δx. Geometric interpretation: signed area under v-t curve gives signed displacement. **Negative areas** (below t-axis) = negative displacement (motion in −x direction). | [xt_vt_at_graphs_atomic, calculus_integration_area_under_curve(math-tools)] | — |  |
| **free_fall_gravity_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Specialisation of constant-a kinematics: a = g downward (= 9.81 m/s² near Earth surface; round to 10 for problems). Drop from rest: v = gt; s = ½gt²; v² = 2gs. Throw up with u: max-height at t = u/g; max-height = u²/(2g); time-of-flight (return to start) = 2u/g. | [kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic, sign_convention_1d_atomic] | [projectile_motion_atomic(T8)] | heavier objects fall faster |
| ↳ feather_coin_vacuum_demo_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Aristotle: heavier falls faster (incorrect). Galileo (~1590): all bodies fall at same rate in absence of air. **Bell-jar + feather + coin demonstration**: with air, coin lands first; with vacuum, both land together. **CBSE Class-11 lab demo** + Apollo-15 hammer-feather on Moon (David Scott, 1971; relevant for Chandrayaan-curious students). | [free_fall_gravity_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ thrown_up_kinematics_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Object thrown up with u (+y up): max-height at t = u/g, v = 0; max-height = u²/(2g); returns with same speed \|u\| at t = 2u/g, v = −u. **Symmetric in time** (up-down) but NOT in distance (max-height reached once). | [free_fall_gravity_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic] | — |  |
| **sign_convention_1d_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Pick a positive direction** (typically +x to the right, +y up). **EVERY quantity carries that sign**: position x, velocity v, acceleration a, force F, displacement Δx. Once chosen, stay consistent throughout the problem. **Foundation rule for all sign-bearing physics quantities downstream** (E, B, I, q, P, M_torque). | [vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | [displacement_vs_distance_atomic, acceleration_definition_atomic, free_fall_gravity_atomic, ALL downstream physics requiring sign] | g is always −9.81 |
| ↳ pick_axis_first_then_substitute_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Worked-example pedagogy: **(a) declare** "+x to the right"; **(b) write** all knowns with explicit sign (u = +5 m/s, a = −2 m/s²); **(c) substitute** into kinematic eq. **Common error**: dropping signs midway through problem. Mandatory checklist nano. | [sign_convention_1d_atomic] | — |  |
| **relative_velocity_1d_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Velocity of A relative to B: **v_AB = v_A − v_B**. If A moves at +30 m/s and B at +20 m/s (both rightward): v_AB = +10 m/s (A appears to recede from B at 10 m/s). If they move toward each other: signs differ → magnitudes add. **1D preview of T7 2D vector relative motion.** | [position_frame_of_reference_atomic, sign_convention_1d_atomic] | [relative_velocity_2d_atomic(T7), boat_river_problem(T7), train_problem_classical_kinematics] | subtract speeds, ignore direction |
| ↳ train_passing_observer_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two-train passing: same-direction (Mumbai-Delhi Rajdhani 110 km/hr passes goods 50 km/hr): relative speed = 60 km/hr; time to pass = (sum of lengths)/(rel speed). Opposite-direction: speeds add. **Classic Indian-railway-context problem.** | [relative_velocity_1d_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ pursuit_problem_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Pursuer at distance d, speed v_p; target at speed v_t (same direction, v_p > v_t). Catch-up time = d / (v_p − v_t). **Common JEE/NEET pattern.** | [relative_velocity_1d_atomic] | — |  |

## T7 — Kinematics 2D Relative Motion  *(Class 11)*

*5 atomic + 16 nano = 21 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **vector_kinematics_2d_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Position **r⃗(t) = x(t) î + y(t) ĵ**; velocity **v⃗ = dr⃗/dt = (dx/dt) î + (dy/dt) ĵ**; acceleration **a⃗ = dv⃗/dt**. **Componentwise principle**: in absence of constraints, x-motion and y-motion are INDEPENDENT — each obeys 1D kinematics separately. | [vector_resolution_atomic(T5), position_frame_of_reference_atomic(T6), avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic(T6), acceleration_definition_atomic(T6)] | [galilean_transformation_atomic, boat_river_crossing_atomic, projectile_motion_atomic(T8), uniform_circular_motion_atomic(T10)] |  |
| ↳ 2d_kinematic_equations_componentwise_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Same three kinematic equations as 1D applied independently to x and y: v_x = u_x + a_x·t; v_y = u_y + a_y·t; x = x₀ + u_x·t + ½·a_x·t²; y = y₀ + u_y·t + ½·a_y·t². **No new formulas** — just two parallel applications of 1D machinery. | [vector_kinematics_2d_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic(T6)] | — | 2D needs new formulas |
| ↳ parametric_2d_trajectory_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Eliminate t between x(t) and y(t) → trajectory y(x). For constant a⃗: trajectory is parabola in general. **Geometric vs kinematic dual representation.** Bridge to T8 projectile-motion trajectory derivation. | [vector_kinematics_2d_atomic, 2d_kinematic_equations_componentwise_nano] | [projectile_trajectory_parabola(T8)] |  |
| ↳ vector_displacement_magnitude_direction_nano | nano | ✅ |  | \|Δr⃗\| = √(Δx² + Δy²); direction θ = arctan(Δy/Δx). **Pythagoras + arctan applied to displacement**. Same idea applies to velocity, acceleration. | [vector_kinematics_2d_atomic, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — |  |
| **galilean_transformation_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Two inertial frames S and S' with S' moving at constant velocity v⃗_S'S relative to S. **Velocity transformation:** v⃗_PS = v⃗_PS' + v⃗_S'S (velocity of particle in S = velocity in S' + velocity of S' wrt S). **Acceleration is invariant:** a⃗ same in all inertial frames. **Time is invariant** (classical, pre-relativistic). | [vector_kinematics_2d_atomic, position_frame_of_reference_atomic(T6), inertial_vs_noninertial_frame_nano(T6)] | [boat_river_crossing_atomic, rain_apparent_velocity_atomic, newton_laws_in_inertial_frames(T11)] |  |
| ↳ velocity_subtraction_for_relative_v_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **v⃗_AB = v⃗_A − v⃗_B** (velocity of A in B's frame). Same formula in 1D and 2D. Vector subtraction = magnitude + direction (not just magnitude). | [galilean_transformation_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ acceleration_invariance_inertial_nano | nano | ✅ |  | a⃗_PS = a⃗_PS' (acceleration the same in all inertial frames). **Why F⃗ = ma⃗ holds in any inertial frame** (Newton 1st-law statement). Bridge to T11. | [galilean_transformation_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic] | [newton_1st_law(T11)] |  |
| ↳ time_invariance_classical_nano | nano | — |  | Δt is the same for all inertial observers (classical). **Breaks down in special relativity** (v approaching c) — flagged for V2 advanced extension. For all Indian-curriculum physics (NCERT, JEE, NEET): time-invariance holds. | [galilean_transformation_atomic] | — |  |
| **boat_river_crossing_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Boat velocity v_b in still water; river current v_r downstream. **Resultant velocity** v⃗_actual = v⃗_b + v⃗_r. **Two distinct optimisations:** (a) **Shortest time**: boat heads straight across (perpendicular to bank); time = w/v_b; drift downstream = (v_r·w)/v_b. (b) **Shortest path** (= width w, no drift): boat heads upstream at angle θ such that v_b·sin(θ) = v_r; time = w/(v_b·cos(θ)) > w/v_b. | [galilean_transformation_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — | shortest time = shortest path |
| ↳ shortest_time_drift_calculation_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Shortest time: head perpendicular. **Crossing-time t = w / v_b** (independent of river current). **Drift x = v_r · t = (v_r · w) / v_b**. Lands x downstream of starting point. Goa-river-tourism-context-anchor. | [boat_river_crossing_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ shortest_path_angle_calculation_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Shortest path (no drift): need v⃗_actual perpendicular to current → v_b·sin(θ) = v_r → **θ = arcsin(v_r / v_b)**. Requires v_b > v_r (else cannot avoid drift). Crossing time = w / (v_b · cos(θ)) > w/v_b. **Slower crossing trades time for zero drift.** | [boat_river_crossing_atomic, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — |  |
| ↳ feasibility_v_b_greater_v_r_nano | nano | ✅ |  | If v_b ≤ v_r: shortest-path solution doesn't exist (boat cannot make headway upstream). Boat drifts regardless of heading. **Real-world example**: Brahmaputra in monsoon (current ~10 km/hr, slow boat 5 km/hr) — boats CANNOT cross straight; drift is unavoidable. | [boat_river_crossing_atomic, shortest_path_angle_calculation_nano] | — |  |
| **rain_apparent_velocity_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Rain falls with v⃗_rain (typically vertical, downward); observer moves with v⃗_obs (typically horizontal). **Apparent rain velocity** as seen by observer = v⃗_apparent = v⃗_rain − v⃗_obs (Galilean transformation in observer's frame). **Apparent direction** makes angle θ with vertical: tan(θ) = v_obs / v_rain. | [galilean_transformation_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic] | — | umbrella-tilt-direction |
| ↳ umbrella_tilt_into_apparent_rain_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **Tilt umbrella INTO direction of apparent rain** (toward direction of motion). Common mis-intuition: "tilt umbrella backward to keep dry" — WRONG; that tilts away from rain. **Mandatory diagram**: stationary observer sees vertical rain → walks east → apparent rain tilts from east-down → umbrella tilts to east. | [rain_apparent_velocity_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ change_of_direction_when_observer_reverses_nano | nano | ✅ |  | If observer reverses direction, apparent-rain direction also reverses (mirror image about vertical). **Indian-monsoon biking anchor**: when biking east in rain, tilt umbrella east; when biking west, tilt umbrella west. Reversal demo. | [rain_apparent_velocity_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ when_observer_runs_horizontal_only_nano | nano | ✅ |  | If v_rain is vertical and v_obs is horizontal: apparent-rain angle from vertical = arctan(v_obs / v_rain). Pure vertical rain (v_obs = 0) → no tilt. Faster observer → larger tilt. Cognitive scaffold for arctan-relationship. | [rain_apparent_velocity_atomic] | — |  |
| **classical_relative_motion_archetypes_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Catalog of canonical relative-motion problem archetypes: (1) train-passenger-platform (Indian Railways daily), (2) aircraft-wind-correction (IndiGo navigation), (3) boat-river-crossing (covered separately as atomic), (4) rain-umbrella (covered separately), (5) cricket-fielder-running-to-catch (sports), (6) two-objects-meeting (general). All use **v⃗_AB = v⃗_A − v⃗_B**. | [galilean_transformation_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ train_platform_passenger_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Passenger walks on moving train. Velocity of passenger in ground frame = velocity of passenger in train frame + velocity of train in ground frame. **v⃗_PG = v⃗_PT + v⃗_TG.** Vande Bharat at 130 km/hr; passenger walking at 5 km/hr (forward in train) → 135 km/hr in ground frame. **Indian-Railways anchor.** | [classical_relative_motion_archetypes_atomic, galilean_transformation_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ aircraft_wind_correction_nano | nano | ✅ |  | IndiGo aircraft Delhi→Bengaluru: heading H ≠ track T because crosswind. v⃗_aircraft-ground = v⃗_aircraft-air + v⃗_air-ground (wind). Pilot must adjust heading to "crab" into wind. **IMD wind-velocity data + flight-planning anchor.** | [classical_relative_motion_archetypes_atomic, galilean_transformation_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ cricket_fielder_running_to_catch_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Cricket ball travelling toward fielder at relative velocity (ball − fielder). Fielder runs in direction such that apparent-velocity vector is straight at them. **Predictive interception** = constant-bearing rule (ball appears stationary in fielder's field of view). IPL fielding-training drill. | [classical_relative_motion_archetypes_atomic, galilean_transformation_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ two_objects_meeting_time_to_collision_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two objects with velocities v⃗_A, v⃗_B and positions r⃗_A, r⃗_B. Relative position r⃗_AB = r⃗_A − r⃗_B; relative velocity v⃗_AB = v⃗_A − v⃗_B. They meet when r⃗_AB(t) = 0. **JEE/NEET problem-template** for two-particle kinematics. | [classical_relative_motion_archetypes_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic] | — |  |

## T8 — Projectile Motion  *(Class 11)*

*5 atomic + 12 nano = 17 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **projectile_decoupling_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | A projectile's motion decomposes into TWO INDEPENDENT motions: **horizontal** (uniform, a_x = 0, x = u_x·t) and **vertical** (free-fall, a_y = −g, y = u_y·t − ½gt²). The only link is the shared time t. **Neither component affects the other.** | [vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7), free_fall_gravity_atomic(T6), 2d_kinematic_equations_componentwise_nano(T7)] | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic, horizontal_launch_atomic, range_height_tof_atomic] | horizontal & vertical motions affect each other |
| ↳ drop_vs_shoot_simultaneity_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Bullet fired horizontally + bullet dropped from same height at same instant: **both hit the ground at the same time** (vertical motion identical; horizontal motion irrelevant to fall-time). Classic demonstration of decoupling. **CBSE Class-11 conceptual-favourite + Mythbusters viral demo.** | [projectile_decoupling_atomic, horizontal_launch_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ monkey_hunter_demo_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Hunter aims directly AT a monkey hanging from a branch; monkey drops at the instant of firing. **Dart hits monkey** regardless of dart speed — because both dart and monkey fall by the same ½gt² in the same time. Decoupling masterclass. | [projectile_decoupling_atomic] | — |  |
| **angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Launch at angle θ to horizontal with speed u: **x = u·cosθ·t**; **y = u·sinθ·t − ½gt²**. Eliminate t → **trajectory: y = x·tanθ − gx²/(2u²cos²θ)** = downward parabola. Symmetric about the peak. | [projectile_decoupling_atomic, parametric_2d_trajectory_nano(T7)] | [range_height_tof_atomic, projectile_on_incline_atomic] |  |
| ↳ parabola_symmetry_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Ascending half (launch→peak) is mirror-image of descending half (peak→landing) on level ground. Time-up = time-down = u·sinθ/g. Speed at any height on the way up = speed at the same height on the way down (directions differ). | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ two_projectile_relative_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two projectiles launched simultaneously: in the frame of one, the other moves in a **STRAIGHT LINE at constant velocity** (relative acceleration = (−g) − (−g) = 0). Elegant simplification; collision-condition reduces to straight-line geometry. | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic, galilean_transformation_atomic(T7)] | — |  |
| **range_height_tof_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | From decoupling on level ground: **Time-of-flight T = 2u·sinθ/g**; **Max-height H = u²sin²θ/(2g)**; **Range R = u²sin(2θ)/g**. Max range at **θ = 45°** (sin2θ = 1). Complementary angles (θ and 90°−θ) give the SAME range. | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic, projectile_decoupling_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic(T6)] | — | range max at θ=90° |
| ↳ complementary_angles_same_range_nano | nano | ✅ |  | θ = 30° and θ = 60° give the same range (sin60° = sin120°). Trade-off: 30° = flatter, faster, lower; 60° = higher, slower, same horizontal distance. Cricket-fielder-throw vs lob choice. | [range_height_tof_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ max_range_45_derivation_nano | nano | ✅ |  | R = u²sin(2θ)/g is maximised when sin(2θ) = 1 → 2θ = 90° → θ = 45°. R_max = u²/g. **Only true on level ground** (incline changes optimal angle — see projectile_on_incline). | [range_height_tof_atomic, angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic] | [projectile_on_incline_atomic] |  |
| ↳ velocity_at_top_nano | nano | ✅ |  | At max-height: **v_y = 0** but **v_x = u·cosθ ≠ 0**. Total speed at top = u·cosθ (purely horizontal). **cognitive_error_target:** "velocity is zero at the top" → only vertical component; horizontal persists. Classic JEE/NEET trap. | [range_height_tof_atomic, projectile_decoupling_atomic] | — |  |
| **horizontal_launch_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Projectile launched HORIZONTALLY from height h with speed u: **time-to-ground t = √(2h/g)** (independent of u — pure free-fall vertically); **horizontal range = u·√(2h/g)**; lands with v_x = u, v_y = √(2gh). Trajectory = half-parabola. | [projectile_decoupling_atomic, free_fall_gravity_atomic(T6)] | — |  |
| ↳ time_independent_of_launch_speed_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two balls launched horizontally from the same height at different speeds (10 m/s and 50 m/s): **both land at the same time** (t = √(2h/g)); the faster one lands farther horizontally. Direct consequence of decoupling. | [horizontal_launch_atomic, projectile_decoupling_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ table_edge_ball_roll_off_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Ball rolling off a table edge at speed u: classic CBSE/ISC physics-practical (measure table-height + landing-distance → compute g or u). **Indian physics-lab-standard experiment.** | [horizontal_launch_atomic] | — |  |
| **projectile_on_incline_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Projectile launched up/down an incline of angle α. **Rotated-axis method**: take axes along + perpendicular to incline; gravity components g·sinα (along, decelerating) + g·cosα (perpendicular). Range up-incline R_up = 2u²·sin(θ−α)·cosθ / (g·cos²α); max-range angle on incline = 45° + α/2 (up) or 45° − α/2 (down). | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic, max_range_45_derivation_nano, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — | max-range angle always 45° |
| ↳ rotated_axis_method_nano | nano | ✅ |  | On an incline, choose x' along the slope + y' perpendicular. Then g resolves into −g·sinα (x') and −g·cosα (y'). Projectile lands when y' = 0 again. **Reduces incline-projectile to standard decoupling in rotated frame.** | [projectile_on_incline_atomic, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — |  |
| ↳ artillery_range_table_application_nano | nano | — |  | Artillery + DRDO ballistic computations use elevation-angle vs range tables (corrected for air-resistance + terrain-slope). The incline-projectile + max-range-angle physics underpins range-table construction. **Indian-Army + DRDO Pokhran anchor.** | [projectile_on_incline_atomic, range_height_tof_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ air_resistance_real_projectile_nano | nano | — |  | Ideal projectile (parabola, symmetric) vs real projectile (asymmetric trajectory — steeper descent, shorter range, lower peak) due to air drag ∝ v². Cricket-ball swing + golf-ball dimples + artillery drag-correction. **Deferred to V2 advanced.** | [angular_projectile_trajectory_atomic] | — |  |

## T9 — Motion In Plane Circular Kinematics  *(Class 11)*

*4 atomic + 11 nano = 15 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **angular_kinematic_variables_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | **Angular position θ** (radians, measured from reference radius); **angular velocity ω = dθ/dt** (rad/s); **angular acceleration α = dω/dt** (rad/s²). Direct rotational analogs of linear position/velocity/acceleration (T6). For rotation, these describe the motion compactly (one variable θ vs two x,y). | [avg_vs_instantaneous_velocity_atomic(T6), acceleration_definition_atomic(T6), vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7)] | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic, tangential_radial_acceleration_atomic, angular_kinematics_rotational(T15)] | ω in degrees/sec |
| ↳ angular_kinematic_equations_nano | nano | ✅ |  | For constant α: **ω = ω₀ + αt**; **θ = ω₀t + ½αt²**; **ω² = ω₀² + 2αθ**. **Identical form to T6 linear equations** (x→θ, v→ω, a→α). Same derivation; same problem-solving decision-tree. Reinforces linear-angular structural analogy. | [angular_kinematic_variables_atomic, kinematic_equations_constant_a_atomic(T6)] | — |  |
| ↳ linear_angular_relations_nano | nano | ✅ |  | **v = ωr** (tangential speed); **a_tangential = αr**; **arc-length s = rθ**. Convert between angular description (compact) and linear description (intuitive). Foundation for T15 rotational mechanics (every linear quantity has an angular analog). | [angular_kinematic_variables_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7)] | [rotational_mechanics_atomics(T15)] |  |
| ↳ radian_measure_why_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Radians (not degrees) make v = ωr and s = rθ clean (no conversion factor). 1 rad = arc-length equal to radius; full circle = 2π rad. **All physics formulae assume radians.** Common student error: plugging degrees into ω formulae. | [angular_kinematic_variables_atomic] | — |  |
| **centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Even at CONSTANT SPEED, circular motion is ACCELERATED because the velocity DIRECTION continuously changes. The acceleration points toward the centre: **a_c = v²/r = ω²r = vω**. This is a purely KINEMATIC fact (geometric consequence of direction-change) — it exists BEFORE any discussion of what force causes it (that's T10). | [angular_kinematic_variables_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7), acceleration_definition_atomic(T6)] | [centripetal_force_atomic(T10), shm_as_circular_projection(T17)] | constant speed = zero acceleration |
| ↳ delta_v_points_inward_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Geometric proof: draw velocity vectors v⃗₁, v⃗₂ at two nearby points on the circle. Δv⃗ = v⃗₂ − v⃗₁ points toward the centre. As Δt→0, a⃗ = Δv⃗/Δt is exactly centripetal. **Diamond-candidate animation**: rotating velocity-vector + Δv⃗-construction showing inward direction. | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic, vector_kinematics_2d_atomic(T7)] | — |  |
| ↳ a_c_three_forms_nano | nano | ✅ |  | a_c = v²/r (linear-speed form) = ω²r (angular form) = vω (mixed). All equivalent via v = ωr. **Use whichever the problem gives.** Decision-tree pedagogy (mirrors T6 kinematic-equation choice). | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic, linear_angular_relations_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ constant_speed_not_constant_velocity_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Speed \|v⃗\| = constant; velocity v⃗ = changing (direction rotates). **Acceleration ≠ 0** despite constant speed. The deepest conceptual point of circular kinematics. **cognitive_error_target** countermeasure: explicit speed-vs-velocity distinction animation. | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic] | — |  |
| **tangential_radial_acceleration_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | For NON-UNIFORM circular motion (changing speed): total acceleration has TWO perpendicular components: **a_tangential = dv/dt = rα** (along velocity, changes SPEED) + **a_radial = v²/r = ω²r** (toward centre, changes DIRECTION). **a_total = √(a_t² + a_r²)**; direction makes angle arctan(a_t/a_r) with the radius. | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic, angular_kinematic_variables_atomic, vector_resolution_atomic(T5)] | — | acceleration in circular motion is always centripetal |
| ↳ speeding_car_on_curve_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Car accelerating while rounding a bend: a_tangential (speeding up along the path) + a_radial (centripetal, turning). Total acceleration tilts forward-of-radius. **Indian-driving anchor**: accelerating out of a curve on Mumbai-Pune Expressway. | [tangential_radial_acceleration_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ uniform_is_special_case_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Uniform circular motion = the special case where a_tangential = 0 (constant speed). Only a_radial (centripetal) remains. Non-uniform is the general case; uniform is the simplification. Cognitive-hierarchy scaffold. | [tangential_radial_acceleration_atomic, centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic] | — |  |
| **uniform_circular_motion_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | UCM = circular motion at CONSTANT speed. Characterised by: constant ω; constant a_c = v²/r (magnitude constant, direction rotating); **period T = 2πr/v = 2π/ω**; **frequency f = 1/T = ω/(2π)**. The canonical kinematic template for all rotational phenomena. | [centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic, angular_kinematic_variables_atomic] | [shm_as_circular_projection(T17), satellite_orbit_kinematics(T16)] | uniform = no acceleration |
| ↳ period_frequency_ucm_nano | nano | ✅ |  | T = 2πr/v = 2π/ω; f = 1/T = ω/(2π); ω = 2πf (angular frequency). **Bridge to T17 SHM**: projection of UCM onto a diameter = SHM (same ω). **Bridge to T16**: satellite orbital period from UCM + gravity (T16 owns the dynamics). | [uniform_circular_motion_atomic, angular_kinematic_variables_atomic] | [shm_atomic(T17)] |  |
| ↳ ceiling_fan_turntable_anchor_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Ceiling-fan (~300-350 rpm = ω ≈ 31-37 rad/s); record-player/turntable (33⅓ rpm); washing-machine-spin (~1400 rpm). Everyday Indian-household UCM anchors. Compute ω, v at blade-tip, a_c. | [uniform_circular_motion_atomic, period_frequency_ucm_nano] | — |  |
| ↳ satellite_circular_orbit_kinematics_nano | nano | ✅ |  | ISRO geostationary satellite (GSAT series): orbital period 24 hr → ω = 2π/(24×3600) rad/s; orbital radius ~42,164 km → v ≈ 3.07 km/s; a_c = v²/r (the KINEMATIC fact; T16 supplies that gravity PROVIDES this a_c). Pure-kinematics version; dynamics in T16. | [uniform_circular_motion_atomic, centripetal_acceleration_kinematic_atomic] | [orbital_dynamics(T16)] |  |

## T11 — Newtons Laws  *(Class 11)*

*15 atomic + 18 nano = 33 concepts.*

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |
|---|---|:---:|:---:|---|---|---|---|
| **newton_first_law_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | A body at rest stays at rest, in uniform motion stays in uniform motion, UNLESS net external force acts; operationally defines an inertial frame | [vector_resolution(T5)] | [newton_second_law_atomic, pseudo_force_atomic, equilibrium_of_particle_atomic] |  |
| ↳ inertial_vs_non_inertial_frames_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Inertial frame: 1st law holds. Non-inertial: 1st law fails → must add pseudo-forces. Earth's surface is approximately inertial for most problems. | [newton_first_law_atomic] | [pseudo_force_atomic] | 1st law fails in moving cars |
| ↳ galilean_invariance_nano | nano | — |  | All inertial frames are equivalent; F = ma in one inertial frame holds in any other with the same form. Foundation of pre-relativistic mechanics | [newton_first_law_atomic, inertial_vs_non_inertial_frames_nano] | — |  |
| **newton_second_law_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | F_net = ma; in vector form ΣF = m·a; central quantitative law of mechanics | [newton_first_law_atomic, vector_resolution(T5), vector_addition(T5)] | [newton_third_law_atomic, work_energy_theorem(T13), gravity_force_atomic, normal_force_atomic, friction_force_atomic, connected_bodies_atomic, block_on_incline_atomic, simple_harmonic_motion(T17), first_law_atomic(T26)] |  |
| ↳ f_eq_dp_dt_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Generalised form: F = dp/dt = d(mv)/dt; reduces to F=ma when m constant; needed for rockets, falling chains, conveyor-belt-sand-loading problems | [newton_second_law_atomic, calculus_derivative(math-tools)] | [variable_mass_rocket_nano, kinetic_theory_pressure_atomic(T27)] | F=ma is universal |
| ↳ impulse_change_in_momentum_nano | nano | ✅ |  | J = ∫F dt = Δp; impulse equals change in momentum. Carom-strike, cricket-bat-ball collision time-integrals all use this | [newton_second_law_atomic, f_eq_dp_dt_nano, calculus_integration(math-tools)] | [collision_atomic(T14)] |  |
| ↳ variable_mass_rocket_nano | nano | ✅ |  | ISRO PSLV/GSLV: M dv/dt = u·(dm/dt) − Mg; thrust = u·(rate of fuel ejection); cross-bridge to T14 momentum-conservation and Tsiolkovsky equation | [newton_second_law_atomic, f_eq_dp_dt_nano] | — |  |
| **newton_third_law_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Every action force has an equal-and-opposite reaction force; the action-reaction PAIR acts on TWO DIFFERENT bodies | [newton_second_law_atomic] | [tension_force_atomic, connected_bodies_atomic, conservation_of_momentum_atomic(T14)] | action and reaction on same body |
| ↳ action_reaction_on_different_bodies_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Side-by-side diagram: hand pushes wall (force ON wall by hand); wall pushes hand (force ON hand by wall). Both forces act, but on different bodies → they DO NOT cancel. **Cognitive-error-prevention nano** (mandatory per NL-G4). | [newton_third_law_atomic] | — | nano |
| ↳ walking_rowing_swimming_examples_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Walking: foot pushes earth back; earth pushes foot forward → person accelerates. Rowing: oar pushes water back; water pushes oar forward. Swimming: hand pushes water back; water pushes hand forward. **Three Indian everyday examples** | [newton_third_law_atomic] | — |  |
| **gravity_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | F_g = mg downward on Earth's surface (g ≈ 9.8 m/s²); more generally F = GMm/r²; mass × gravitational-field-strength | [newton_second_law_atomic] | [normal_force_atomic, tension_force_atomic, block_on_incline_atomic, gravitation(T16)] |  |
| **normal_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Contact force perpendicular to the surface, magnitude adjusts to whatever keeps body from penetrating surface (it's NOT always equal to mg) | [newton_second_law_atomic, gravity_force_atomic] | [friction_force_atomic, free_body_diagram_atomic, block_on_incline_atomic] | N = mg always |
| ↳ normal_force_in_lift_problem_nano | nano | ✅ |  | In accelerating lift: N = m(g + a) upward acceleration; N = m(g − a) downward acceleration; N = 0 free-fall (passenger feels weightless). **Everyday Indian elevator examples** | [normal_force_atomic, newton_second_law_atomic] | — |  |
| **tension_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Force transmitted along a string/rope; tension is uniform along massless inextensible string; tension at any point = force pulling along string at that point | [newton_second_law_atomic, newton_third_law_atomic] | [connected_bodies_atomic, mechanical_constraints_atomic] | tension equals weight always |
| ↳ massless_inextensible_string_constraint_nano | nano | ✅ |  | String mass = 0 → tension uniform along string. String inextensible → tangential velocity along string is same at every point. These are TWO separate idealisations bundled into "ideal string." | [tension_force_atomic, mechanical_constraints_atomic] | — |  |
| **friction_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Force opposing relative motion or tendency of motion between contacting surfaces; static μ_s (no relative motion yet) vs kinetic μ_k (motion occurring); cross-link to T12 for full derivation | [newton_second_law_atomic, normal_force_atomic] | [block_on_incline_atomic, friction(T12 full treatment)] |  |
| **spring_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | F = −kx (Hooke's law); restoring force proportional to displacement from natural length; sign convention "minus" indicates restoring | [newton_second_law_atomic] | [simple_harmonic_motion(T17)] |  |
| **applied_external_force_atomic** | atomic | — |  | Any non-internal force from outside the system (push, pull, applied tension, applied gravity-field). The "F" in F=ma typically refers to applied + reaction-from-environment | [newton_second_law_atomic] | [free_body_diagram_atomic, equilibrium_of_particle_atomic] |  |
| **pseudo_force_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | In non-inertial frame with acceleration a_frame: pseudo-force F_pseudo = −m·a_frame acts on every mass m in the frame. Real to observer in that frame; vanishes in inertial frame. | [newton_first_law_atomic, inertial_vs_non_inertial_frames_nano, newton_second_law_atomic] | [centrifugal_force_nano, train_acceleration_nano] | pseudo-force isn't real |
| ↳ centrifugal_force_nano | nano | ✅ |  | In rotating frame: F_centrifugal = mω²r outward; explains why water-bucket-on-string works; classic NCERT/HCV example. Equivalent to centripetal-force-balance from inertial frame view. | [pseudo_force_atomic, circular_motion(T10)] | — |  |
| ↳ train_acceleration_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Passenger in accelerating train (a_train forward): pseudo-force m·a_train backward on passenger; passenger leans back. Indian Railways everyday experience. | [pseudo_force_atomic] | — |  |
| **free_body_diagram_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Cognitive tool: isolate ONE body, draw ALL external forces ACTING ON IT, set up F_net = ma equations. THE universal mechanics workflow. | [newton_second_law_atomic, gravity_force_atomic, normal_force_atomic, tension_force_atomic, friction_force_atomic] | [block_on_incline_atomic, connected_bodies_atomic, every-mechanics-application] | drawing forces ON other bodies in same FBD |
| ↳ fbd_step_by_step_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Step 1: identify body. Step 2: list all forces ACTING ON it (gravity, normal, tension, friction, applied, spring). Step 3: choose axes. Step 4: resolve. Step 5: write ΣF_x = ma_x and ΣF_y = ma_y. | [free_body_diagram_atomic] | — |  |
| **equilibrium_of_particle_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Particle in equilibrium: ΣF = 0 → ΣF_x = 0 AND ΣF_y = 0; static equilibrium or dynamic-equilibrium (constant velocity); equivalent to 1st law special case | [newton_first_law_atomic, free_body_diagram_atomic, vector_resolution(T5)] | [tension_in_strings_at_a_point_nano] |  |
| ↳ tension_in_strings_at_a_point_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two strings joined at a point with weight hanging: ΣF_x = 0 (horizontal tension components cancel); ΣF_y = 0 (vertical components support weight). Classic JEE example. | [equilibrium_of_particle_atomic, tension_force_atomic, vector_resolution(T5)] | — |  |
| **mechanical_constraints_atomic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Equations relating motion of different connected bodies: string-inextensibility, pulley-massless, rigid-body, surface-rigidity. Each constraint reduces # of independent variables. | [tension_force_atomic, newton_second_law_atomic] | [connected_bodies_atomic, block_on_incline_atomic] |  |
| ↳ pulley_constraint_v_dot_equal_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Single fixed pulley + inextensible string: \|v₁\| = \|v₂\| but in opposite directions; sign convention via constraint equation x₁ + x₂ = L. Differentiate twice to get a₁ = −a₂. | [mechanical_constraints_atomic] | [connected_bodies_atomic] |  |
| **connected_bodies_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Two or more bodies connected by string/rod/pulley; apply F=ma to each body separately + constraint equation; combine for system solution | [newton_second_law_atomic, free_body_diagram_atomic, tension_force_atomic, mechanical_constraints_atomic] | [block_on_incline_atomic, atwood_machine_sim_nano] |  |
| ↳ atwood_machine_sim_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two masses m₁ + m₂ over single fixed pulley: a = g(m₁−m₂)/(m₁+m₂); T = 2gm₁m₂/(m₁+m₂). Asymmetric acceleration is visually compelling. | [connected_bodies_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ block_on_table_connected_to_hanging_mass_nano | nano | ✅ |  | m₁ on table, string over pulley at edge, m₂ hangs: a = m₂g/(m₁+m₂); if friction on table, a = (m₂ − μm₁)g/(m₁+m₂). Classic JEE template. | [connected_bodies_atomic, friction_force_atomic] | — |  |
| **block_on_incline_atomic** | atomic | ✅ | 💎 | Block of mass m on incline angle θ: gravity component along incline = mg sin θ; perpendicular = mg cos θ; if μ < tan θ slides; if μ ≥ tan θ static | [newton_second_law_atomic, free_body_diagram_atomic, friction_force_atomic, vector_resolution(T5)] | [connected_bodies_atomic] |  |
| ↳ angle_of_repose_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Angle θ at which block just starts sliding: tan θ = μ_s; defines the static-friction limit on incline | [block_on_incline_atomic, friction_force_atomic] | — |  |
| ↳ connected_blocks_on_double_incline_nano | nano | ✅ |  | Two blocks on opposite faces of same wedge connected by string over pulley at apex: classic IIT-Advanced problem | [block_on_incline_atomic, connected_bodies_atomic] | — |  |

| Concept ID | Type | Sim | Diamond | Description (what it teaches) | Requires | Required-by | Targets misconception |

| **friction_as_contact_force_component** | atomic | ✅ |  | Friction is the parallel component of contact force (normal = perpendicular component, friction = parallel) | `contact_forces`, `normal_reaction`, `vector_resolution` | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion, friction_block_on_inclined_plane, block_against_vertical_wall_friction |  |

| **static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction** | atomic | ✅ |  | Two regimes: not-sliding (static) and sliding (kinetic) | friction_as_contact_force_component | kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N, static_friction_self_adjusting, mu_static_greater_than_mu_kinetic, friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion, static_friction_drives_motion |  |
| ↳ impending_motion_concept | nano | ✅ |  | "Would-move-if-not-for-friction" scenario; static friction acts on bodies at rest with applied force | friction_as_contact_force_component | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, static_friction_self_adjusting, angle_of_repose |  |
| ↳ relative_velocity_check_for_kinetic | nano | ✅ |  | Slipping ⇒ kinetic regime; no slipping ⇒ static regime | `relative_velocity` (Topic 5) | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion, conveyor_belt_friction |  |
| **kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N** | atomic | ✅ |  | f_k = μ_k × N (magnitude formula) | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, `normal_reaction` | friction_block_on_horizontal_surface, friction_block_on_inclined_plane, block_sliding_down_rough_incline_acceleration, friction_two_blocks_stacked |  |
| ↳ linear_proportionality_to_N | nano | ✅ |  | f doubles when N doubles (linearity of f-N relation) | kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N | kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N, friction_block_on_horizontal_surface |  |
| **static_friction_self_adjusting** | atomic | ✅ |  | Static friction = whatever force is needed up to limit (variable, not a fixed value) | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, `newton_first_law` (Topic 11) | limiting_static_friction_max_value, static_friction_drives_motion |  |
| ↳ f_s_equals_applied_force_below_limit | nano | ✅ |  | Applied 2 N → friction 2 N; applied 5 N → friction 5 N (up to limit) | static_friction_self_adjusting | static_friction_self_adjusting |  |
| ↳ f_vs_F_applied_graph | nano | ✅ |  | Graph: 45° line until f_max, then drops to μ_k N | static_friction_self_adjusting, limiting_static_friction_max_value, mu_static_greater_than_mu_kinetic | static_friction_self_adjusting |  |
| **limiting_static_friction_max_value** | atomic | ✅ |  | f_s ≤ μ_s N; equality at impending motion | static_friction_self_adjusting, kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N | mu_static_greater_than_mu_kinetic, angle_of_repose, static_friction_drives_motion |  |
| ↳ equality_at_impending_motion | nano | ✅ |  | At the instant motion is about to start, f_s = μ_s N (boundary condition) | limiting_static_friction_max_value | limiting_static_friction_max_value, angle_of_repose |  |
| ↳ transition_static_to_kinetic_at_breakaway | nano | ✅ |  | Overshoot: at breakaway, friction drops from μ_s N to μ_k N → block accelerates | limiting_static_friction_max_value, mu_static_greater_than_mu_kinetic | limiting_static_friction_max_value |  |
| **mu_static_greater_than_mu_kinetic** | atomic | ✅ |  | Empirical: μ_s > μ_k (e.g., steel-steel: μ_s=0.7, μ_k=0.6) | kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N, limiting_static_friction_max_value | static_friction_drives_motion |  |
| **friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion** | atomic | ✅ |  | Friction direction = opposite to **relative** velocity of object w.r.t. surface (not absolute motion!) | static_vs_kinetic_friction_distinction, `relative_velocity` (Topic 5) | static_friction_drives_motion, friction_in_accelerating_frame, conveyor_belt_friction |  |
| ↳ direction_opposite_to_actual_relative_velocity | nano | ✅ |  | For kinetic friction: direction = −v_rel | friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion | friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion |  |
| ↳ direction_opposite_to_impending_relative_velocity | nano | ✅ |  | For static friction: direction = whatever opposes the would-be slip direction | friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion, impending_motion_concept | friction_direction_opposes_relative_motion, angle_of_repose |  |

| **friction_independent_of_contact_area** | atomic | ✅ |  | Counterintuitive: same block on same surface, friction force doesn't depend on which face is down | kinetic_friction_formula_f_equals_mu_k_N, limiting_static_friction_max_value | — |  |
